Expressions That Are Not Oaths: Invoking Wrath or Self-Labeling
Ruling that invoking Allah's wrath or labeling oneself a sinner is not an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish statements that are oaths from those that are not, applying the two conditions: conventional usage (mut'ārif) and eternal sanctity (hurmat al-ism).

Core Idea
A statement invoking changeable prohibitions or self-curses lacks the eternal sanctity needed for an oath.

You already know that an oath involves invoking Allah's name. But what about saying 'May Allah's wrath be upon me' or 'I am a thief'? Are these oaths? The Hanafi school says no, and the reasoning reveals two essential conditions for a valid oath: it must be customary and must involve an eternally sacred attribute. This lesson will help you apply these conditions correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal classification
Determine what the speaker is doing: making an oath, a curse, or a self-accusation? The text rules that these are not oaths (laysa bi-ḥālif).
2
Examine the first reason: invocation of wrath is a self-curse
The phrase 'upon me is Allah's wrath' is a du'ā' (supplication) on oneself, not an oath. An oath must be attached to a condition (al-sharṭ) in a way that binds; here it is merely a wish.
The text says: 'wa lā yataʿallaqu dhālika bi-sh-sharṭ'
3
Examine the second reason: hurmat al-ism
Calling oneself a fornicator, thief, etc., refers to acts whose prohibition is subject to change (abrogation, context). A valid oath must invoke an attribute that is eternally inviolable (ḥurmat al-ism).
Example: zina becomes permissible after marriage; riba may be permissible with a ḥarbī in dār al-ḥarb.
4
Assess conventional usage (mut'ārif)
Even if something could theoretically be an oath, it must be commonly used as such in the community. The given expressions are not conventionally used to swear.
This shows the role of ʿurf in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَ إِنْ فَعَلْتُ كَذَا فَعَلَيَّ غَضَبُ اللهِ أَوْسَخَطُ اللَّهِ فَلَيْسَ بِحَالِفٍ لِأَنَّهُ دَعَا عَلَى نَفْسِهِ وَلَا يَتَعَلَّقُ ذَالِكَ بِالشَّرْطِ وَلَانَّهُ غَيْرُ مُتَعَارِفٍ وَكَذَا إِذَا قَالَ إِنْ فَعَلْتُ كَذَا فَانَازَانِ أَوْ سَارِقُ أَوْ شَارِبُ خَمْرٍ أَواكِلُ رِبْوالَا نَّ حُرِّمَةَ هَذِهِ الْأَشْيَاءِ تَحْتَمِلُ النَّسْحَ وَالتَّبْدِيلَ فَلَمْ يَكُنْ فِى . حُرمَةِ الْإِسْمِ وَلِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِمُتَعَارِفٍ
And if he says: 'If I do such-and-such, then upon me is the wrath of Allah or the anger of Allah,' he is not swearing, because he is invoking a curse upon himself, and that is not attached to the condition, and because it is not conventionally used. Likewise, if he says: 'If I do such-and-such, then I am a fornicator, or a thief, or a drinker of wine, or an eater of riba,' because the prohibition of these things is subject to abrogation and change, so it is not in the sanctity of the Name, and because it is not conventionally used.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing expressions that do not constitute oaths. The reasoning is twofold: (1) Invoking Allah's wrath (ghadab, sakhat) is a curse on oneself, not an oath; it is not customary as an oath. (2) Calling oneself a sinner (zani, sariq, sharib khamr, akil riba) is not an oath because the prohibited status of these acts is subject to abrogation (naskh) and change (tabdil) – e.g., marriage turns zina into halal, riba with harbis in dar al-harb is permissible. Thus, such statements lack the sanctity of Allah's name (hurmat al-ism), which is eternal and unchangeable. Additionally, these are not conventionally used as oaths. The Hanafi position is clear: for a statement to be an oath, it must involve a divine attribute that is eternally revered and be customary among the people.

Key Terms
حرمة الاسم (hurmat al-ism): The sanctity/inviolability of Allah's name. For a statement to be an oath, it must involve invoking a name or attribute that is eternally sacred and not subject to change.
متعارف (mutta'ārif): Conventionally used/customary. An expression must be customarily recognized as an oath to count as one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'If I go to that party, may Allah's wrath be upon me,' according to Hanafi fiqh, is this an oath?
A) Yes, because it invokes Allah's wrath.
B) Yes, because it is a strong statement.
C) No, because it is a curse on oneself and not conventionally used as an oath.
D) No, because it is not binding.

Key Takeaway
A statement is an oath only if it is conventionally used and invokes an eternally sacred attribute; expressions of self-curse or self-accusation of changeable sins lack these conditions.

Multiple Oaths Require Multiple Expiation
Ruling on multiple oaths and counting oaths in conditional statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that multiple oaths require multiple kaffarah and identify what constitutes a separate oath.

Core Idea
Each broken oath is an independent sin; multiple oaths require multiple expiations.

This lesson covers the fundamental Hanafi principle that an oath is a legal act incurring liability for expiation if broken. When multiple oaths are sworn, each is treated independently—regardless of whether they were made in one sitting or many. The text also clarifies how to count oaths when conditional statements or multiple divine names are used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Find the key statement: 'If someone swears multiple oaths, that many expiations are due.' This applies whether the oaths were sworn in one sitting or multiple.
The number of expiations equals the number of valid oaths broken.
2
Recognize examples of multiple oaths
The text gives examples: saying 'If I do X, I am a Jew, I am a Christian' counts as two oaths. Saying 'Wallah' and 'Allah al-Rahman' (without conjunction) also counts as two. With 'and' (عطف), by consensus it is two.
The conjunction 'and' explicitly separates them.
3
Understand the reason for multiple kaffarah
Each oath is a separate commitment to Allah. Breaking each requires its own kaffarah, even if they are similar or sworn together.
Hanafi law does not combine oaths unless they are explicitly made as one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر کئی قسمیں کھا ئیں تو اتنے ہی کفارے لازم ہوں گے ۔ خواہ ایک مجلس میں قسمیں کھائی ہو یا کسی مجلسوں میں ۔ اگر کوئی یہ کہے کہ اگر میں ایسا کروں تو میں یہودی ہوں میں نصرانی ہوں۔ تو یہ دو قسمیں ہیں ۔ اسی طرح اللہ والہ یا اللہ الرحمن بھی دو قسمیں ہیں۔ یہی اصح ہے ۔ اور اگر عطف کے ساتھ کے یعنی اللہ اور الرحمن و بالاتفاق دو تمہیں ہوئیں ۔ اگر کسی نے کہا کہ مجھے اپنی زندگی کی قسم یا تیری زندگی یا تیرے سر کی قسم تو خوف ہے کہ وہ کافر ہو جائے گا۔ اور اگر اس قسم کے پورے کرنے کا اعتقاد کرے یعنی اس کے دل میں یہ بات ہو کہ اس قسم کی وجہ سے اب مجھے اس قسم کا پورا کرنا واجب ہے۔ تو کافر ہو جائے گا۔ اگر کسی نے کہا کہ اللہ جانتا ہے کہ میں نے ایسا کیا یا میں اللہ کو شاہد کرتا ہوں کہ ایسا کیا یا نہیں کیا یا اللہ کو شاہد کر کے گواہی دے حالانکہ وہ جھوٹا ہے تو زاہدی نے کہا ہے کہ اکثر فقہا کے نزدیک کافر ہو جائے گا۔ اور شمنی نے کہا کہ اصبح یہ ہے کہ کافر نہ ہوگا۔
If someone swears multiple oaths, then that many expiations are due, whether sworn in one sitting or different sittings. If someone says 'If I do this, I am a Jew, I am a Christian,' these are two oaths. Similarly, 'Wallah' and 'Allah al-Rahman' are two oaths; this is the most correct. And if with conjunction, i.e., 'Allah and al-Rahman', by consensus they are two oaths. If someone says 'By my life, or by your life, or by your head,' then it is feared he will become a disbeliever (kafir). And if he believes that he must fulfill this kind of oath, i.e., in his heart he intends it as binding, then he becomes a kafir. If someone says 'Allah knows that I did such-and-such' or 'I make Allah a witness that I did such-and-such or did not do it' or gives testimony making Allah a witness while he is lying, then Zahidi said that according to most fuqaha, he becomes a kafir. And Shumni said that asahh (the most correct) is that he does not become a kafir.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings (masa'il) on various topics related to oaths. The first ruling: multiple oaths require multiple expiations, regardless of whether they were sworn in one sitting or many. Second ruling: if someone says 'If I do X, I am a Jew, I am a Christian,' that counts as two oaths because two independent conditional kufr statements are made. Similarly, saying 'Wallah' and 'Allah al-Rahman' together (without conjunction) are two separate oaths. With the conjunction 'and' (و), they are two by consensus. Third ruling: swearing by one's own life, another's life, or another's head is feared to lead to kufr. According to the text, if the person intends such an oath as legally binding (i.e., believes it makes the action wajib upon himself), he becomes a kafir because he is ascribing divine authority to a created thing. Fourth ruling: saying 'Allah knows that I did X' while lying, or making Allah a witness to a false statement, is a serious matter. Zahidi reports that most fuqaha consider this kufr. However, Shumni asserts that the sahih (correct) view is that it is not kufr. The Hanafi mufta bihi on this point is generally that it is not kufr unless one intended to mock or denigrate Allah's knowledge, but the text presents both opinions.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking an oath; the amount depends on the type of oath.
عطف ('atf): Grammatical conjunction. Here, using 'and' between two divine names makes two separate oaths by consensus.
شاهد (shahid): Witness. Making Allah a shahid to a lie is the subject of dispute regarding kufr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, how many oaths are there in the statement: 'If I do this, I am a Jew, I am a Christian'?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) It depends on intention

Key Takeaway
Multiple oaths require multiple expiations; each conditional kufr statement counts as a separate oath.

The Ruling on Swearing by Life or by a Created Thing
Ruling on swearing by life/head

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when swearing by a created thing leads to kufr according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Swearing by life or head is not a valid oath; if believed binding, it can lead to kufr.

This lesson addresses the sensitive issue of swearing by other than Allah. While some mistakenly think such oaths are valid but sinful, the Hanafi school distinguishes between mere utterance and belief. If one believes the oath is legally binding like an oath by Allah, that belief is tantamount to shirk.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of oath
The text mentions swearing by one's own life, another's life, or someone's head. These are created things.
Valid oaths must be by Allah or His attributes.
2
Understand the feared consequence
The text says 'it is feared he becomes a kafir.' This indicates a strong prohibition; it may not be outright kufr if done ignorantly.
Fear (khawf) indicates a serious sin.
3
Condition for kufr
If the person believes that such an oath carries the same binding obligation as an oath by Allah, then he becomes a kafir. This is because he ascribes divine authority to a created thing.
Intention and belief are decisive.

The Ruling on Saying 'Allah Knows' or Making Allah a Witness While Lying
Ruling on falsely invoking Allah's knowledge/witness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the dispute between Zahidi and Shumni regarding kufr when one falsely invokes Allah's knowledge or testimony.

Core Idea
Invoking Allah's knowledge or testimony to a lie is a major sin; whether it amounts to kufr is disputed among Hanafi scholars.

This lesson deals with a serious matter: using Allah's name to support a falsehood. The text presents two opinions: one that it constitutes kufr (Zahidi) and another that it does not (Shumni, preferred). Understanding the difference helps in issuing fatwa and avoiding takfir where not due.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts in question
Saying 'Allah knows that I did X' when one did not, or making Allah a witness to a false statement.
These are forms of lying about Allah's knowledge or using His name to give false testimony.
2
Present the two opinions
Zahidi reports that most fuqaha consider this kufr. Shumni says the asahh (most correct) view is that it is not kufr.
The difference is whether the person intended to mock or show contempt.
3
Understand the reasoning
Those who say kufr argue that one is attributing false knowledge to Allah or misusing His name as a witness, which is a form of disrespect tantamount to mockery. Those who say not kufr argue that it is a major sin but not necessarily apostasy unless there is clear intention to degrade Allah.
The Hanafi mufta bihi tends to the more lenient view to avoid hasty takfir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Invalid Oath: Invoking Allah's Wrath
First part of the ruling: invalid oath due to curse and non-customary phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a conditional statement invoking Allah's wrath or displeasure is not a valid oath and state the two reasons given in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Invoking Allah's wrath on oneself under a condition is a curse, not a vow, and is not customary — thus not a binding oath.

You might think that any conditional statement that includes Allah's name or attributes counts as an oath. However, the Hanafi school draws a clear line: an oath must be a vow invoking Allah's sanctity in a customary manner. In this lesson, we examine why saying 'If I do X, then upon me be Allah's wrath' does not form a valid oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
The statement is: 'If I do such-and-such, then upon me be Allah's wrath or Allah's displeasure.' Note that it uses Allah's attribute of wrath.
This is a conditional threat against oneself.
2
Recognize the first reason: it is a curse, not a vow
The text states he is 'invoking a curse upon himself.' An oath must be a binding commitment (e.g., 'By Allah, I will not do X'), not a prayer for harm.
A curse does not create legal obligation.
3
Recognize the second reason: it is not customary
The phrase 'upon me be Allah's wrath' is not a customary form of swearing in the language. Custom (urf) determines whether a phrase is recognized as an oath.
Customary usage is crucial in Hanafi law.
4
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, this statement does not make him a halif (oath-taker). No kaffarah is due if the condition is fulfilled.
No legal effect from such a statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يكون يمينا و ما لا يكون يمينا وَلَوْ قَالَ إِنْ فَعَلْتُ كَذَا فَعَلَيَّ غَضَبُ اللهِ أَوْ سَخَطُ اللَّهِ فَلَيْسَ بِحَالِفٍ لِأَنَّهُ دَعَا عَلَى نَفْسِهِ وَلَا يَتَعَلَّقُ ذَلِكَ بِالشَّرْطِ وَلِأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُتَعَارِفٍ وَكَذَا إِذَا قَالَ إِنْ فَعَلْتُ كَذَا فَأَنَا زَانٍ أَوْ سَارِقٌ أَوْ شَارِبُ خَمْرٍ أَوْ آكُلُ رِبًا لِأَنَّ حُرْمَةَ هَذِهِ الْأَشْيَاءِ تَحْتَمِلُ النَّسْخَ وَالتَّبْدِيلَ فَلَمْ يَكُنْ فِي حُرْمَةِ الْإِسْمِ وَلِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِمُتَعَارِفٍ
Chapter on what constitutes an oath and what does not. And if he says, 'If I do such-and-such, then upon me be Allah's wrath or Allah's displeasure,' he is not an oath-taker because he is invoking a curse upon himself and it does not relate to the condition, and because it is not customary. And similarly if he says, 'If I do such-and-such, then I am an adulterer, or a thief, or a wine-drinker, or a usurer,' because the prohibition of these things is subject to abrogation and change, so it is not in the category of the sanctity of the Name, and because it is not customary.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage from the Hidayah clarifies two categories of conditional statements that do not constitute a valid oath (yamin). First, if a person says, 'If I do X, then may Allah's wrath or displeasure be upon me,' this is not an oath because: (a) it is a prayer against oneself, not a statement binding oneself to an action; (b) such a phrase is not commonly used as an oath in the language. Second, if a person says, 'If I do X, then I am an adulterer, thief, wine-drinker, or usurer,' this also is not an oath. The reason is that the prohibition of these acts (zina, theft, drinking wine, usury) is not immutable—they could change in different contexts (e.g., marriage makes halal what was haram in zina; war booty in dar al-harb permits usury). Therefore, these statements do not carry the weight of the sanctity of Allah's name, which is eternal and unchangeable. Hence, no oath is formed, and no kaffarah is required for breaking them. The Urdu commentary further explains that if one says 'I am a Jew or Christian' as a condition, that does become an oath because it implies rejecting faith, which is a form of reverence to Allah? The key principle: an oath must invoke something sacred (divine name or attribute) and must be a customary form of swearing.

Key Terms
يمين (yamīn): An oath that invokes Allah or His attributes to affirm or deny an action, creating a binding obligation.
حالف (ḥālif): A person who takes an oath
غضب الله (ghaḍab Allāh): Allah's wrath
متعارف (mut'ārif): Customary, commonly used

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hidayah, why is saying 'If I do X, upon me be Allah's wrath' not a valid oath?
A) Because it does not mention Allah's name explicitly.
B) Because it is a curse upon oneself and not customary.
C) Because it is not conditional.
D) Because it uses the word 'wrath' which is disrespectful.

Key Takeaway
A statement that calls down divine wrath upon oneself under a condition is not an oath because it is a curse and not customary.

Invalid Oath: Conditional Sinful Acts
Second part of the ruling: invalid oath due to mutable prohibition and non-customary phrasing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why conditional statements naming a sinful act (e.g., 'I am an adulterer') do not constitute valid oaths and differentiate them from valid oath formulas.

Core Idea
Naming a prohibited act in a conditional statement does not form an oath because the prohibition is changeable and such phrasing is not customary.

Some people might think that vowing to commit a sin if they break their word constitutes a binding oath. However, the Hanafi school rules that saying 'If I do X, I am an adulterer, a thief, a wine-drinker, or a usurer' does not create an oath. Why? Because the prohibition of these acts can change (e.g., marriage makes zina halal; in some contexts usury may be permitted) and because such expressions are not customary as oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statements
The statements are: 'If I do X, then I am an adulterer, or a thief, or a wine-drinker, or a usurer.' These label oneself with a sinful act.
These are self-declarations, not invocations of Allah.
2
Understand the first reason: prohibition is not immutable
The text says 'the prohibition of these things is subject to abrogation and change.' For example, zina becomes halal through marriage; theft may be permitted in war booty; usury is said to be permissible in dar al-harb. Therefore, the act's prohibition is not eternal like Allah's name.
Sanctity of the Name is eternal; sinful acts are not.
3
Understand the second reason: not customary
These phrases are not customary forms of swearing. Customary oaths invoke Allah or sacred concepts in a recognized structure.
Custom (urf) is key.
4
Apply to other similar statements
Any conditional self-labeling with a prohibited act that is changeable will not form an oath. The ruling is the same.
This covers many potential phrases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يكون يمينا و ما لا يكون يمينا وَلَوْ قَالَ إِنْ فَعَلْتُ كَذَا فَعَلَيَّ غَضَبُ اللهِ أَوْ سَخَطُ اللَّهِ فَلَيْسَ بِحَالِفٍ لِأَنَّهُ دَعَا عَلَى نَفْسِهِ وَلَا يَتَعَلَّقُ ذَلِكَ بِالشَّرْطِ وَلِأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُتَعَارِفٍ وَكَذَا إِذَا قَالَ إِنْ فَعَلْتُ كَذَا فَأَنَا زَانٍ أَوْ سَارِقٌ أَوْ شَارِبُ خَمْرٍ أَوْ آكُلُ رِبًا لِأَنَّ حُرْمَةَ هَذِهِ الْأَشْيَاءِ تَحْتَمِلُ النَّسْخَ وَالتَّبْدِيلَ فَلَمْ يَكُنْ فِي حُرْمَةِ الْإِسْمِ وَلِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِمُتَعَارِفٍ
Chapter on what constitutes an oath and what does not. And if he says, 'If I do such-and-such, then upon me be Allah's wrath or Allah's displeasure,' he is not an oath-taker because he is invoking a curse upon himself and it does not relate to the condition, and because it is not customary. And similarly if he says, 'If I do such-and-such, then I am an adulterer, or a thief, or a wine-drinker, or a usurer,' because the prohibition of these things is subject to abrogation and change, so it is not in the category of the sanctity of the Name, and because it is not customary.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage from the Hidayah clarifies two categories of conditional statements that do not constitute a valid oath (yamin). First, if a person says, 'If I do X, then may Allah's wrath or displeasure be upon me,' this is not an oath because: (a) it is a prayer against oneself, not a statement binding oneself to an action; (b) such a phrase is not commonly used as an oath in the language. Second, if a person says, 'If I do X, then I am an adulterer, thief, wine-drinker, or usurer,' this also is not an oath. The reason is that the prohibition of these acts (zina, theft, drinking wine, usury) is not immutable—they could change in different contexts (e.g., marriage makes halal what was haram in zina; war booty in dar al-harb permits usury). Therefore, these statements do not carry the weight of the sanctity of Allah's name, which is eternal and unchangeable. Hence, no oath is formed, and no kaffarah is required for breaking them. The Urdu commentary further explains that if one says 'I am a Jew or Christian' as a condition, that does become an oath because it implies rejecting faith, which is a form of reverence to Allah? The key principle: an oath must invoke something sacred (divine name or attribute) and must be a customary form of swearing.

Key Terms
يمين (yamīn): An oath that invokes Allah or His attributes to affirm or deny an action, creating a binding obligation.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation; the changing of a legal ruling by a later revelation.
متعارف (mut'ārif): Customary, commonly used

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hidayah, why does saying 'If I do X, I am a thief' not constitute a valid oath?
A) Because theft is not a major sin.
B) Because the prohibition of theft is subject to abrogation and it is not customary.
C) Because it does not mention Allah explicitly.
D) Because it is not conditional enough.

Key Takeaway
Conditional self-labeling with a sinful act does not form an oath because the prohibition is mutable and the phrase is not customary.

Expiation for Breaking an Oath: Options and the Shafi'i Dispute
States the three kaffarah options and the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three options for kaffarat al-yamin and explain the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether the options are sequential.

Core Idea
The word 'aw indicates choice among three equal options for kaffarah; fasting only when unable.

This passage from the Hidayah explains the mandatory expiation for breaking a valid oath. The Quran presents three distinct actions: freeing a slave, clothing ten poor persons, or feeding them. The central legal question is whether these options are strictly ordered or freely chosen. The Hanafi school holds that any one suffices, while Imam al-Shafi'i sees absolute freedom. We will examine the textual proof and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three kaffarah options and their conditions
List the three actions: freeing a slave (with conditions like being believing and able to work), clothing ten poor persons (minimum covering 'awrah), and feeding ten poor persons (average family food). Note that you may choose any one.
The conditions are the same as in zihar expiation for the slave.
2
Understand the Quranic evidence and the word 'aw'
The verse (5:89) uses 'aw' meaning 'or', indicating that any one of the three fulfills the expiation. This is the Hanafi basis for free choice among the three.
The verse also mentions fasting for those who cannot afford any of the three.
3
Contrast the Hanafi and Shafi'i readings
Hanafi: the three options are equally valid if able; fasting only if unable. Shafi'i: absolute freedom to choose even if able. The Hanafi response cites a different recitation (qira'ah) that supports their view.
The dispute hinges on whether 'aw' implies equal choice or whether the context restricts fasting to inability.
4
Examine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Consider a wealthy person: Hanafi says they cannot fast as expiation; Shafi'i says they could. The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position.
This affects validity of the expiation act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الْكَفَّارَةِ قَالَ كَفَّارَةُ الْيَمِينِ عِتْقُ رَقَبَةٍ يُجْرَى فِيهَا مَا يُجْزِئُ فِي الظِّهَارِ وَإِنْ شَاءَ كَسَا عَشَرَةَ مَسَاكِينَ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ ثَوْبًا فَمَا زَادَ وَأَدْنَاهُ مَا يَجُوزُ فِيهِ الصَّلَاةُ وَإِنْ شَاءَ أَطْعَمَ عَشَرَةَ مَسَاكِينَ كَالْإِطْعَامِ فِي كَفَّارَةِ الظِّهَارِ وَالْأَصْلُ فِيهِ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى فَكَفَّارَتُهُ إِطْعَامُ عَشَرَةِ مَسَاكِينَ الْآيَةُ وَكَلِمَةُ أَوْ لِلتَّخْيِيرِ فَكَانَ الْوَاجِبُ أَحَدَ الْأَشْيَاءِ الثَّلَاثَةِ قَالَ فَإِنْ لَمْ يَقْدِرْ عَلَى أَحَدِ الْأَشْيَاءِ الثَّلَاثَةِ صَامَ ثَلَاثَةَ أَيَّامٍ مُتَتَابِعَاتٍ وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ يُخَيَّرُ لِإِطْلَاقِ النَّصِّ وَلَنَا قِرَاءَةُ
Section on expiation. He said: The expiation of an oath is the freeing of a slave; the same conditions apply as in zihar expiation. And if he wishes, he may clothe ten poor persons, each with a garment, and he may give more; the minimum is what suffices for valid prayer. And if he wishes, he may feed ten poor persons, the same as feeding in zihar expiation. The source for this is the saying of Allah Most High: 'So its expiation is feeding ten poor people...' and the word 'or' indicates choice, so one of the three things is obligatory. He said: If he is unable to do any of the three things, he should fast three consecutive days. And al-Shafi'i said he is given choice because of the absolute wording of the text; and we have the recitation.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage from the Hidayah (citing al-Quduri) details the expiation (kaffarah) for breaking an oath. The ruling: one must choose one of three actions: (1) freeing a slave who meets the same criteria as in zihar expiation (i.e., a believing slave, sound, able to work), (2) clothing ten poor persons with at least one garment each that would cover the 'awrah for prayer, or (3) feeding ten poor persons with the average food one gives to one's family. The foundational evidence is the Quranic verse (Quran 5:89): 'So its expiation is feeding ten poor people... or clothing them or freeing a slave.' The word 'or' (aw) indicates free choice (takhyeer), so the obligated person may select any of three. If one is unable to do any of these due to poverty, then one must fast three consecutive days. The text then notes a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i: Shafi'i holds that the order in the verse (feeding then clothing then freeing) is not sequential, and one has absolute choice even if able to do all; but the Hanafi position (supported by a different recitation of the verse?) maintains the choice is among the three. The Urdu commentary clarifies that clothing must cover the 'awrah, food must be average, and slave must be serviceable.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation required for breaking a valid oath (yamin).
عتق رقبة (ʿitq raqabah): Freeing a slave who meets the same conditions as in zihar expiation (believing, sound, able to work).
كسوة (kiswah): Clothing given to a poor person; minimum must cover the 'awrah for prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A robe covering the whole body
B) A garment that covers the 'awrah properly for prayer
C) A cap and shawl
D) A two-piece suit

Key Takeaway
Expiation for breaking an oath is wajib; one must choose among freeing a slave, clothing ten poor, or feeding ten poor; fasting is only for the unable.

Phrases That Do Not Form an Oath
Explaining that invocations like 'wrath of Allah' are not oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which expressions do not constitute a valid yamin and state the Hanafi criteria for an oath.

Core Idea
An oath requires expressing veneration of Allah; curses or self-recriminations are not oaths.

You may have heard people say things like 'May the wrath of Allah be upon me if I lie' or 'If I do this, I am a thief.' Are these binding oaths? In this lesson, we classify such expressions according to Hanafi fiqh and learn why they do not create a legal obligation to perform expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression
Read the statement and note whether it is a supplication (du'a) against oneself or a self-ascription of a prohibited act.
Examples: 'May Allah be angry with me' is a du'a; 'I am a fornicator' is self-ascription.
2
Check for veneration of Allah
Assess whether the expression shows the greatness of Allah (ta'zim). An oath must contain a form of veneration, not a curse.
If it is a curse, it lacks ta'zim.
3
Check customary usage
Consider whether the expression is commonly used as an oath in the language and culture. If it is not customary, it does not form an oath.
Even if it contains Allah's name, if it is not customary, no oath is formed.
4
Determine the ruling
If either veneration or custom is absent, the expression does not form a yamin. The speaker is not a halif, and no expiation is due upon breach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسی طرح اس نے یوں کہا کہ اگر میں ایسا کروں تو مجھے پراللہ کا غضب یا اللہ کی ناراضگی ہو پس وہ حالف نہ ہوگا کیونکہ اس نے اپنے آپ پر بد دعا کی اور اس کا تعلق شرط سے نہیں ہے اور اسلئے کہ اس کا رواج نہیں اور اسی طرح اگر کسی نے کہا اِنْ فَعَلْتَ كَذَا فَأَنَازَانِ أَوْسَارِقٌ أَوْ شَارِبُ خَمْرٍ او اكل ربا کیونکہ ان اشیاء کی حرمت نخ تبدیل کے قابل ہے اسلئے یہ سم اللہ کی حرمت کے معنی نہیں ہیں اور اسلئے بھی کہ یہ تم رائج نہیں ہے۔ تشریح قسم چونکہ اللہ تعلی کی عظیم ظاہر کرنے کیلئے ہوتی ہے اس میں حرمت الہی ہوتی ہے اور جن الفاظ میں یہ حرمت الہی پائی جائے اس سے یمین منعقد ہو گی ان الفاظ مذکورہ یعنی غضب اللہ [بخط؟] اللہ و غیرہ میں یہ معنی نہیں اسلئے یمین نہ ہوگی۔
Similarly, if he said: 'If I do such-and-such, may the wrath of Allah be upon me or may Allah be displeased with me,' then he is not a swearer because he made a supplication against himself, and it is not connected to the condition, and because it is not customary. Similarly, if someone said: 'If you do such-and-such, then I am a fornicator, or a thief, or a wine-drinker, or an usurer,' because the prohibition of these things is not subject to change, so these do not carry the meaning of the sanctity of Allah's name, and also because this is not customary. Explanation: Since an oath is to show the greatness of Allah, it contains the sanctity of Allah. Words that contain this sanctity form an oath. These mentioned words, i.e., 'ghadab Allah', [bikhat?] Allah, etc., do not contain this meaning, so no oath is formed.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining which expressions do not constitute a legally binding oath (yamin) in the Hanafi school. The author states that if a person says, 'If I do such-and-such, then may the wrath of Allah be upon me' or 'may Allah be displeased with me,' this is not an oath. The reason is that such a statement is a du'a (supplication) against oneself, not an expression of veneration for Allah. Furthermore, such expressions are not customary as oaths. Similarly, if someone says, 'If you do such-and-such, then I am a fornicator, a thief, a wine-drinker, or an usurer,' this is also not an oath because these acts are inherently prohibited and their prohibition cannot be used to express the sanctity of Allah's name; moreover, such usage is not customary. The underlying principle is that an oath (yamin) is meant to show the greatness of Allah, so it must involve invoking the sanctity of Allah's name. Expressions that are curses or self-recriminations do not achieve this purpose. Thus, they do not form a valid oath.

Key Terms
یمین (yamin): A legally binding oath that requires expiation if broken
حالف (halif): The person who swears an oath
غضب اللہ (ghadab Allah): Wrath of Allah; an expression of divine anger

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) If I do this, may the wrath of Allah be upon me.
C) By Allah, I will do it.
D) If I do this, I have made a vow to give charity.

Key Takeaway
Expressions that are supplications against oneself or self-ascription of sins do not form oaths because they lack veneration of Allah.

Expiation of an Oath: Three Options and a Dispute on Consecutive Fasting
Ruling on oath expiation with a dispute about consecutive fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three options for oath expiation, identify the condition of consecutiveness for fasting, and explain the Hanafi proof for consecutiveness.

Core Idea
Expiation requires one of three specific acts; fasting is only valid if consecutive.

This lesson examines the expiation (kafarah) of a broken oath as laid out in the Hanafi school. We begin with the three options from the Quran and then focus on a key dispute: must the fasting option be consecutive? We will see how the Hanafi school uses a companion reading to specify the general text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three expiation options
List the three options: freeing a slave, clothing ten poor persons, or feeding ten poor persons. Note that each has conditions similar to zihar expiation.
The Quranic verse (5:89) presents these as alternatives.
2
Understand the minimum clothing requirement
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Muhammad says minimum covers the 'awrah for prayer; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say it must cover most of the body, making trousers alone insufficient.
The relied-upon view is the latter, as the wearer would be called 'naked' in custom.
3
Analyze the dispute on fasting consecutiveness
Al-Shafi'i argues fasting can be non-consecutive because the Quranic text is absolute. The Hanafi school relies on Ibn Mas'ud's qira'ah that adds 'consecutive days', treating it like a well-known hadith that can specify the general.
This dispute shows how a companion reading can function as a proof for takhsis in Hanafi usul.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud is authoritative like a mashhur hadith. Thus the condition of consecutiveness is established, and non-consecutive fasting does not fulfill kafarah.
The thamarah: if someone fasts non-consecutively, he must repeat with consecutive days.
5
Consider the timing of expiation
The Hanafi school holds that expiation given before breaking the oath is invalid because expiation is to conceal a crime, and no crime has occurred yet. Al-Shafi'i disagrees. However, once given, the wealth becomes charity and cannot be taken back.
This illustrates the principle that expiation follows the violation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اتنی مقدار ہو جس سے نماز ہو جائے یعنی مرد کیلئے اس کا ستر چھپ جائے اور عورت کے چہرے ہاتھ اور پاؤں کے علاوہ سارا جسم ڈھانپا جا سکے اور کھانا کھلانا ہے تو دس مسکینوں کو اوسط درجہ کا کھانا کھلائے اور اگر غلام آزاد کرنا ہے تو ایسا غلام آزاد کرے کہ اس سے خدمت کی جا سکتی ہو اگر اس غلام سے خدمت لینا ناممکن یا دشوار ہو تو اس غلام کو آزاد کرنا درست نہیں اس طرح مد براور مکاتب کو بھی آزاد کرنا درست نہیں کہ ان میں ملکیت ناقص ہے۔ کفارہ کی اشیاء ثلاثہ پر قادر نہ ہو تو مسلسل تین روزے رکھے قَالَ فَإِنْ لَمْ يَقْدِرُ عَلَى أَحَدِ الْأَشْيَاءِ الثَّلَثَةِ صَامَ ثَلَثَةَ أَيَّامٍ مُتَتَابِعَاتٍ وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ يُخَيَّرُ لِاطْلَاقِ النَّصِّ وَلَنَا قِرَاءَةُ ابْنِ مَسْعُودٍ فَصِيَامُ ثَلَثَةِ أَيَّامٍ مُتَتَابِعَاتٍ وَهِيَ كَالْخَبَرِ الْمَشْهُورِ ثُمَّ الْمَذْكُورُ فِي الْكِتَابِ فِي بَيَانِ أَدْنَى الْكِسْوَةِ مَرْوِيٌّ عَنْ مُحَمَّدٍ وَعَنْ أَبِى يُوسُفَ وَأَبِي حَنِيْفَةَ أَنَّ أَدْنَاهُ مَا يَسْتُرُ عَامَّةَ بَدَنِهِ حَتَّى لَا يَجُوزَ السَّرَاوِيْلُ وَهُوَ الصَّحِيحُ لِأَنَّ لَابِسَهُ يُسَمَّى عُرْيَانًا فِي الْعُرْفِ لَكِنَّ مَالَا يُجْزِيْهِ عَنِ الْكِسْوَةِ يُجْزِيْهِ عَنِ الطَّعَامِ بِاعْتِبَارِ الْقِيْمَةِ ترجمه قدوری نے فرمایا کہ اگر ان تین چیزوں میں سے کوئی چیز بھی نہ دے سکتا ہو تو متواتر تین روزے رکھے۔ اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا ہے کہ اسے روزوں کے رکھنے میں اتنا اختیار ہے کہ اگر چاہے تو انہیں متواتر رکھ لے اور اگر چاہے تو متفرق کر کے رکھے۔ کیونکہ نص مطلق ہے اس میں پے در پے کی کوئی قید نہیں ہے۔ اور ہماری دلیل حضرت عبداللہ بن مسعود رضی اللہ تعالیٰ عنہ کی قرائت ہے۔ فصِيَامُ ثَلَثَةِ أَيَّامٍ مُتَتَابِعَاتٍ یعنی اس قراءت میں متتابعات (پے درپے ہونے) کی قید ہے۔ اور یہ قراءت حدیث مشہور کے مثل ہے۔ یعنی اس کی بناء پر قرآن پر زیادتی کرنا جائز ہے۔ پھر کتاب میں جوادنی درجہ کپڑے کا ذکر کیا ہے وہ امام محمد سے مروی ہے۔ اور امام ابو حنیفہ وابو یوسف نے کہا ہے کہ کپڑے کا ادنی درجہ یہ ہے کہ بدن کے اکثر حصہ کو چھپا دے۔ اس لئے صرف پانجامہ دینا جائز نہیں ہوگا۔ اور یہی قول صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ صرف پائجامہ پہننے والے کو عرف میں نگا کہتے ہیں۔ لیکن اتنی رقم کہ اس سے کپڑا خرید کر دینے سے اس کا لباس ادا نہ ہوتا ہو اس سے قسمت کے لحاظ سے کھانا دیدینا جائز ہوگا۔ تشرح .... وعن ابى يوسف .... الخ امام ابوحنیفہ وابو یوسف نے کہا ہے کہ کپڑا ادا کرنے میں کم از کم اتنا ہونا چاہئے جو بدن کے اکثر حصہ کو چھپا لے اس لئے صرف پائجامہ دینے سے کفارہ ادا نہیں ہوگا۔ یہی قول صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ صرف پائجامہ پہننے والے کو عرف میں بنگا کہا جاتا ہے۔لیکن جس رقم سے صرف اتنا کپڑا خریدا جا سکے جو اکثر بدن کو نہ چھپا سکے مگر اس سے کھانا دینا ہو جائے تو بلحاظ قیمت کے ادا ہو جائے گا۔ فائدہ یعنی مثلا کسی کے پاس صرف دس روپے ہیں جن سے دس آدمیوں کا کھانا بخوبی ادا ہو سکتا ہو لیکن لباس دینے میں تھیں روپے خرچ ہوتے ہوں اور اس نے لباس ہی کی نیت سے دس مسکینوں کو میں روپے دیدئے تو لباس کا کفارہ ادا نہ ہو گا۔ مگر کھانے کی قیمت ان کو پہنچ کر کھانے سے کفارہ ادا ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن یہ حکم اس صورت میں ہوگا جبکہ کھانے کی جگہ کھانے کی قیمت دینی بھی جائز ہو۔ جیسا کہ ہمارا مذ ہب ہے۔ ....... حث پر کفارہ کو مقدم کرنا وَإِنْ قَدَّمَ الْكَفَّارَةَ عَلَى الْحِنْثِ لَمْ يُجْزِهِ وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ يُجْزِيْهِ بِالْمَالِ لِأَنَّهُ أَدَّاهَا بَعْدَ السَّبَبِ وَهُوَ الْيَمِينُ فَاشْبَهَ التَّكْفِيرَ بَعْدَ الْجَرْحِ وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْكَفَّارَةَ لِسَتْرِ الْجَنَايَةِ وَلَا جِنَايَةَ هَهُنَا وَالْيَمِينُ لَيْسَتْ بِسَبَبٍ لَا نَّهُ مَانِعٌ غَيْرُ مُفْضِ بِخِلَافِ الْجَرْحِ لِأَنَّهُ مُفْضِ ثُمَّ لَا يُسْتَرَدُّ مِنَ الْمِسْكِينِ لِوُقُوْعِهِ صَدَقَةٌ ترجمہ اور اگر تم کھانے والے نے اپنے حانث ہونے سے پہلے ہی کفارہ دید یا تو جائز نہیں ہوگا ۔ امام شافعی نے فرمایا ہے کہ مال سے کفارہ دیدینا جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ اس میں کفارہ کا سبب یعنی قسم پائے جانے کے بعد کفارہ ادا کیا ہے۔ اس لئے حکم ایسا ہوا جیسے کسی کو زخمی کرنے کے بعد اس کے مرنے سے پہلے ہی کفارہ کا غلام آزاد کر دیا ۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ جرم چھپانے کے لئے کفارہ ہوتا ہے۔ اور یہاں قسم توڑنے سے پہلے کوئی جرم نہیں ہے۔ اور تم کفارہ کا سبب نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ قسم تو رو کنے والی ہوتی ہے پہنچانے والی نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ یعنی قسم اس لئے ہوتی ہے کہ آدمی اسے پورا کرنے اور اس لئے نہیں ہوتی ہے کہ اسے تو ڑ کر کفارہ ادا کرے۔ اس بناء پر قسم کفارہ تک پہنچانے کا سبب نہیں ہے۔ بخلاف زخم کے کہ وہ تو موت تک پہنچانے والا ہوتا ہے۔ پھر دینے والا اس مال کو واپس نہیں لے سکتا ہے کیونکہ وہ صدقہ ہو گیا۔ (اور صدقہ کا واپس لینا جائز نہیں ہے)۔ تشریح وَلَنَا أَنَّ الْكَفَّارَةَ لِسَتْرِ الْجَنَايَةِ وَلَا جِنَايَةَ هَهُنَا وَالْيَمِينُ لَيْسَتْ بِسَبَبٍ لَا نَّهُ مَانِعٌ غَيْرُ مُفْ الخ اگر تم توڑنے سے
He said: The expiation of an oath is freeing a slave, concerning which what is valid in zihar is applied. If he wishes, he may clothe ten poor persons, each one a garment; more is possible, and its minimum is what is permissible for prayer. If he wishes, he may feed ten poor persons like the feeding in zihar expiation. The basis for this is the saying of Allah Most High: 'The expiation is feeding ten poor persons...' the verse. The word 'or' is for choice, so one of the three things is obligatory. ... He said: If he is unable to do any of the three things, he fasts three consecutive days. Al-Shafi'i said he has a choice (to fast consecutively or not) due to the absolute text. Our proof is the reading of Ibn Mas'ud: 'fasting three consecutive days,' and this is like a well-known hadith. Then what is mentioned in the book regarding the minimum of clothing is narrated from Muhammad, and from Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa that its minimum is what covers most of his body, so that trousers are not sufficient, and this is the correct view because the wearer is called naked in custom. But what is not sufficient for clothing is sufficient for food by considering the value. ... And if he gives expiation before breaking the oath, it is not valid. Al-Shafi'i said it is valid (if given) in wealth because he performed it after the cause, which is the oath, so it resembles expiation after a wound. Our proof is that expiation is to conceal the crime, and here there is no crime. The oath is not a cause because it is a preventer, not a means. Unlike a wound, which is a means. Then, it cannot be taken back from the poor because it has become a charity.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling on the expiation (kafarah) of an oath (yamin), with proofs and disputes. The author begins by stating the three options for kafarah: freeing a slave, clothing ten poor persons, or feeding them. The conditions for the slave are the same as in zihar expiation. For clothing, the minimum is a garment that covers the 'awrah sufficient for prayer. For feeding, it is the same as zihar expiation. The primary proof is the Quranic verse (Al-Ma'idah 5:89) which presents these three options as alternatives, indicated by the word 'aw' (or) giving choice. The text then discusses inability: if one cannot afford any of the three, he must fast three consecutive days. A dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i arises: al-Shafi'i allows non-consecutive fasting because the verse does not specify consecutiveness. The Hanafi position relies on the qira'ah (variant reading) of Ibn Mas'ud which explicitly says 'consecutive days,' and this reading is treated like a well-known hadith (khabar mashhur) that can specify the general Quranic text. Next, the minimum clothing definition is clarified: according to Imam Muhammad, the minimum is what covers the 'awrah for prayer, but according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, it must cover most of the body, so trousers alone are insufficient; this is the relied-upon view. However, if the available money is insufficient for clothing but sufficient for food, it may be used for food by considering the value. Finally, the passage addresses the issue of paying kafarah before breaking the oath. The Hanafi school holds it invalid because expiation is meant to conceal a crime (jinayah), and no crime has occurred yet; the oath itself is a preventer (mani') not a means (mufdi) leading to expiation, unlike a wound that can lead to death. Al-Shafi'i considers it valid because the cause (the oath) exists. The Hanafi response emphasizes the purpose of expiation. However, once given, the wealth becomes charity and cannot be taken back.

Key Terms
كفارة (kafarah): Expiation or atonement for breaking an oath
عتق رقبة (itq raqabah): Freeing a slave; one valid option for kafarah
كسوة (kiswah): Clothing given to ten poor persons as kafarah
حانث (hanith): The person who breaks an oath (yamin)
قِرَاءَة ابْنِ مَسْعُود (qira'at Ibn Mas'ud): The variant reading of the Quran attributed to Ibn Mas'ud, which includes the word 'مُتَتَابِعَاتٍ' (consecutive) in the fasting verse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person cannot afford any of the three main options for oath expiation, what must he do?
A) Fast three non-consecutive days.
B) Fast three consecutive days.
C) Give money equivalent to clothing.
D) Feed sixty poor persons.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires fasting to be consecutive for oath expiation, based on the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud.

Paying Kaffarah Before Breaking the Oath: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Dispute on advance kaffarah before oath violation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that paying kaffarah before breaking an oath is invalid and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Kaffarah conceals sin; no sin before oath-breaking. Oaths are for fulfilling, not for breaking.

This lesson examines a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over whether kaffarah (expiation) for an oath can be paid before the oath is actually broken. The Shafi'is consider it valid by analogy with a fatal wound, while the Hanafis insist that kaffarah must follow the sin it conceals. Understanding this disagreement reveals fundamental principles about the purpose of expiation and the nature of oaths in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that paying kaffarah before breaking an oath (before hanth) is invalid; it counts only as sadaqah.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and their proof
The Shafi'is argue it is valid because the cause (sabab) of kaffarah, the oath itself, already exists. They analogize to a fatal wound where kaffarah can be paid before death by consensus.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis reject the analogy: a wound inevitably leads to death, but an oath does not inevitably lead to breaking; one may keep it lifelong.
4
Trace the Hanafi dalil
Kaffarah is to conceal sin (jurm); no sin exists before hanth. The oath is for fulfillment, not for deliberate breaking.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis treat advance payment as sadaqah not kaffarah; Shafi'is count it as valid kaffarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
پہلے ہی اس کا کفارہ ادا کر دیا جائے تو احناف کے نزدیک جائز نہ ہوگا۔ لیکن شوافع کے نزدیک مال سے کفارہ دینا جائز ہوگا۔ کیونکہ کفارہ کا سبب جو تم ہے وہ تو کفارہ دینے سے پہلے ہی پایا گیا ہے۔ اس کی مثال ایسی ہوگی کہ کسی زخمی کے مرجانے سے پہلے ہی اس کے کفارہ میں غلام آزاد کر دیا جائے تو بالاتفاق جائز ہوتا ہے۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ کفارہ دینے کا مقصد جرم کو چھپانا ہوتا ہے اور ہمارے اس قسم کے مسئلہ سے پہلے تو کوئی جرم نہیں پایا گیا ہے بلکہ صرف قسم پائی گئ ہے جبکہ تم کفارہ واجب ہونے کا سب نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ قسم کی چیز کو بدلنے والی ہوتی ہے۔ کفارہ تک پہنچانے والی نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ یعنی قسم اس لئے ہوتی ہے کہ آدمی اسے پوری کرے۔ اور اس لئے نہیں ہوتی ہے کہ اسے خواہ مخواہ ہی توڑ کر کفارہ ادا کرے۔ بخلاف زخم کے کہ اس کا آخر انجام موت ہی ہے۔ یعنی زخمی ہو کر آخر کار مرجاتا ہے۔ فائدہ یعنی زخمی کرنے میں مجروح کی موت سے پہلے کفارہ دینے کو اس لئے جائز کہا گیا ہے کہ کاری اور مہلک زخم کا انجام موت ہی ہے۔ بخلاف قسم کے کہ اس کا انجام کفارہ ہی نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ کوئی شخص قسم کھا کر زندگی بھر اپنی قسم پر جائز وناجائز ) باقی رہ جائے تو اس پر کفارہ لازم نہیں ہو گا۔ اور اگر کوئی حانث ہونے سے پہلے کفارہ ادا کر دے تو وہ کفارہ میں شمار نہ ہو گا۔ البتہ صدقہ ہو جائے گا۔
If one pays its kaffarah beforehand, then according to the Hanafis it will not be permissible. But according to the Shafi'is, paying kaffarah from wealth will be permissible, because the cause (sabab) of kaffarah, which is the oath, has already existed before paying kaffarah. An example of this is: if before the death of a wounded person, one frees a slave as kaffarah for him, it is permissible by agreement. And our proof is that the purpose of paying kaffarah is to conceal the sin, and in our case of this type of issue, no sin has occurred yet; rather only the oath has occurred, while the oath is the cause of kaffarah being obligatory. Because the oath is something that changes [the action] and does not lead to kaffarah. That is, the oath is taken so that a person fulfills it, and it is not taken so that he breaks it unnecessarily and pays kaffarah. Unlike the wound, whose final outcome is death. That is, after being wounded, he eventually dies. Benefit: In causing a wound, paying kaffarah before the death of the wounded is considered permissible because a fatal wound's outcome is death. Unlike the oath, whose outcome is not kaffarah, because a person can swear an oath and remain upon his oath, halal or haram, all his life, then kaffarah will not be obligatory upon him. And if one pays kaffarah before committing hanth, that kaffarah will not be counted; however, it will be considered sadaqah.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute (ikhtilaf) between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding paying kaffarah for an oath before actually breaking it. The Shafi'is consider it valid because the cause (sabab) – the oath itself – exists. They analogize it to a fatal wound where kaffarah can be paid before death, which is agreed upon. The Hanafi position, argued here, is that kaffarah is not valid before hanth because kaffarah aims to conceal the sin (jurm). Since no sin (breaking the oath) has occurred, there is no concealment. The oath is meant to be fulfilled, not to be deliberately broken for kaffarah. The wound analogy is rejected because the wound inevitably leads to death, whereas an oath does not inevitably lead to breaking; one can remain faithful throughout life. If one pays kaffarah in advance, it counts only as voluntary sadaqah, not as the required kaffarah. The passage establishes the Hanafi view that kaffarah must follow hanth, not precede it.

Key Terms
كفارہ (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking an oath; it conceals the sin and is obligatory after hanth.
قسم (qasam): An oath; a commitment made by swearing by Allah, meant to be fulfilled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone pays kaffarah for an oath before actually breaking the oath, what is the ruling?
A) It is valid and counts as kaffarah.
B) It is invalid and does not count as kaffarah; it becomes sadaqah.
C) It is permissible only if the oath is on a sin.
D) It is valid only if the person later breaks the oath.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah must follow the sin it expiates; paying beforehand does not fulfill the obligation and is merely sadaqah.

Ruling on Breaking a Sinful Oath
Ruling and proof for breaking a sinful oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on oaths involving sin, apply the hadith evidence, and distinguish between breaking the oath (with kaffarah) and committing the sin.

Core Idea
A sinful oath must be broken and expiated; committing the sin offers no compensation.

We often make impulsive oaths that involve sinning—like vowing not to pray or to cut family ties. This passage from al-Quduri clarifies the Islamic duty: break the sinful oath and pay kaffarah. The Prophet's hadith provides the proof, and the rational justification shows that kaffarah can compensate for breaking the oath, but no compensation exists for the sin itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an oath on a sin (ma'siyah). The Hanafi ruling is that it is wajib to break the oath and pay kaffarah.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) commands: 'Whoever swears an oath and sees something else better, let him do that which is better and then pay kaffarah.'
Hadith is narrated by Muslim and others.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
Breaking the oath is compensated by kaffarah (feeding 10 poor, etc.), but committing the sin (like not praying) has no compensation and remains a sin.
Thus, breaking is the lesser evil.
4
Apply to given examples
Examples: swearing not to pray, not to speak to father, or to kill someone. In each case, the better course is the opposite (pray, speak, don't kill) and then pay kaffarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ عَلَى مَعْصِيَةٍ مِثْلُ اَنْ لَّا يُصَلِّيَ أَوْلَا يُكَلَّمَ آبَاهُ اَوْلَيَقْتُلَنَّ فُلَانًا يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُحْنِتَ نَفْسَهُ وَيُكَفِّرَ عَنْ يَمِينِهِ لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ مَنْ حَلَفَ عَلَى يَمِيْنِ وَرَأَى غَيْرَهَا خَيْرًا مِنْهَا فَلْيَاتِ بِالَّذِي هُوَ خَيْرٌ ثُمَّ لِيُكَفِّرْ عَنْ يَمِينِهِ وَلَانَّ فِيْمَا قُلْنَاهُ تَفْوِيْتُ الْبَرِّ إِلى جَابِرٍ وَهُوَ الْكَفَّارَةً وَ لَا جَابِرَ لِلْمَعْصِيَةِ فِي ضِدِهِ ترجمہ قدوری نے کہا ہے کہ جس شخص نے کسی نافرمانی یا گناہ کے کام پر قسم کھائی مثلاً وہ نماز نہیں پڑھے گا یا اپنے باپ سے گفتگو نہیں کرے گا یا فلاں شخص کو ضرور قتل کرے گا تو اسے چاہئے کہ اپنی ایسی قسم توڑ دے اور اس قسم کا کفارہ ادا کر دے۔ رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے اس فرمان کی وجہ سے کہ جس نے کوئی قسم کھائی اور بعد میں اس کے خلاف کرنے میں ہی بہتری سمجھی تو جسے وہ بہتر سمجھے وہ کر ڈالے پھر اپنی قسم کا کفارہ ادا کردے (مسلم وغیرہ نے اس کی روایت کی ہے ) اور اس دلیل سے کہ جو صورت ہم نے بیان کی ہے اگر اس میں اپنی قسم پوری نہ کر سکے تو کفارہ سے اس کی کچھ تلافی ہو جاتی ہے۔ اور اس کے خلاف کرنے سے یعنی اس معصیت پر عمل کر لینے میں نقصان کے سوا کچھ تلافی نہیں ہوتی ہے۔
He (al-Quduri) said: And whoever swears an oath on a sin, such as that he will not pray, or he will not speak to his father, or he will definitely kill so-and-so, it is incumbent upon him to break his oath and to pay kaffarah for his oath, due to the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever swears an oath and then sees that something else is better than it, let him do that which is better, and then let him pay kaffarah for his oath.' And because in what we have said, there is compensation for breaking the oath, which is the kaffarah, and there is no compensation for the sin in its opposite. Translation: Al-Quduri said: The person who swears an oath on an act of disobedience or sin, for example, he will not pray, or he will not talk to his father, or he will definitely kill so-and-so, then he should break such an oath and pay kaffarah for that oath. This is due to the command of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'Whoever swears an oath and later thinks that doing contrary to it is better, then let him do what he considers better, then pay kaffarah for his oath' (narrated by Muslim and others). And due to the proof that in the scenario we have described, if he cannot fulfill his oath, then kaffarah compensates for the breaking. But doing the contrary, i.e., actually committing the sin, has no compensation except harm.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on oaths involving sin (ma'siyah). Al-Quduri, in his matn, gives examples: vowing not to pray (which is abandoning an obligation), vowing not to speak to one's father (which is cutting family ties - sila rahim), or vowing to kill someone (which is murder). The ruling is that the person must break (hanth) the oath and then pay kaffarah. The primary proof is the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) reported by Muslim: when one sees a better course, he should take it and then expiate his oath. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that by breaking the oath, one can compensate via kaffarah, which wipes out the sin of breaking the oath. But by actually committing the sin (e.g., not praying), there is no compensation; it remains a sin. Thus, it is mandatory (wajib) to break a sinful oath and pay kaffarah, avoiding the greater sin.

Key Terms
معصیت (ma'siyah): An act of disobedience to Allah, a sin.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath, the act of violating the sworn commitment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears 'By Allah, I will not pray today,' what should he do according to the Hanafi school?
A) He should pray and then pay kaffarah.
B) He should not pray to fulfill his oath.
C) He must keep the oath and not pay kaffarah.
D) He should break the oath only but not pay kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
A sinful oath must be broken and expiated; committing the sin is worse and has no compensation.

Ruling on a Disbeliever's Oath
Ruling on the invalidity of an oath by a disbeliever

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a disbeliever's oath and explain why it is invalid, applying the condition of ta'zim (glorification of Allah).

Core Idea
A disbeliever's oath is void because he cannot truly glorify Allah.

Oaths in Islamic law are solemn commitments made by swearing by Allah, which inherently requires glorifying Him. A disbeliever, by virtue of his disbelief, lacks this capacity for true glorification. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that such an oath is invalid from its inception, and no liability—including kaffarah—arises from breaking it, even if the person later becomes Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the act: a disbeliever swears an oath and later commits hanth (breaking it). The ruling: the oath is batil (void) and no liability (kaffarah or other) applies.
The source text explicitly states 'فَلَاحِنْتَ عَلَيْهِ' (nothing is upon him).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is a ta'lil (reasoning): an oath requires glorification of Allah; a disbeliever is not qualified for that. Also, kaffarah is an act of worship, which a disbeliever is not qualified to perform.
No explicit Quran or Hadith is cited; the reasoning is based on the nature of oaths.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The link: an oath is 'ma'qud li-ta'zim Allah' (concluded for glorification of Allah). A disbeliever lacks iman and thus cannot have genuine ta'zim. Therefore, the oath is not validly concluded.
The term 'تعظیم' (ta'zim) is the key condition.
4
Note the cross-madhhab agreement
This ruling is muttafaq alayh (unanimous among schools); no ikhtilaf is mentioned. However, the focus here is the Hanafi reasoning.
The source indicates no intra- or inter-madhhab dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا حَلَفَ الْكَافِرُ ثُمَّ حَنَكَ فِي حَالِ كُفْرِهِ أَوْ بَعْدَ إِسْلَامِهِ فَلَاحِنْتَ عَلَيْهِ لِأَنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِأَهْلِ لِلْيَمِينِ لِأَنَّهَا تُعْقَدُ لِتَعْظِيمِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَمَعَ الْكُفْرِ لَايَكُونُ مُعَظَمَـا وَلَاهُوَ أَهْلَّ لِلْكَفَّارَةِ لِأَنَّهَا عِبَادَةٌ ترجمہ اور اگر کسی کافر نے قسم کھائی پھر وہ حانث ہو گیا خواہ حالت کفر میں ہو یا اسلام لانے کے بعد ہو تو قسم توڑنے کی وجہ سے اس پر کچھ لازم نہیں ہو گا۔ کیونکہ قسم کھانے کے وقت اس کو قسم کھانے کی اہلیت نہیں تھی۔ اس لئے قسم تو اللہ تعالیٰ کی تعظیم کے لئے کھائی جاتی ہے۔ اور کافر رہتے ہوئے اسے تعظیم کی اہلیت نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ اور نہ ہی وہ کفارہ ادا کرنے کی لیاقت رکھتا ہے۔ کیونکہ کفارہ عبادت ہے۔
And if a disbeliever swears an oath and then commits hanth, whether in the state of his disbelief or after his Islam, there is no liability upon him, because he is not qualified for an oath. For an oath is made for the glorification of Allah the Exalted, and with disbelief, he does not glorify, nor is he qualified for kaffarah because it is an act of worship. Translation: And if a disbeliever swears an oath, then he breaks it, whether in the state of disbelief or after accepting Islam, nothing becomes obligatory upon him due to breaking the oath. Because at the time of swearing, he did not have the capacity to swear an oath. For an oath is sworn for the glorification of Allah the Exalted, and while being a disbeliever, he does not have the capacity for glorification. Nor is he capable of paying kaffarah, because kaffarah is worship.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on oaths made by a disbeliever (kafir). The rule is that such an oath is legally invalid. The reason (ta'lil) is that an oath is a solemn commitment made by swearing by Allah, which inherently involves glorifying (ta'zim) Allah. A disbeliever does not truly glorify Allah at the moment of the oath because of his disbelief. Therefore, the oath is not validly concluded, and no hanth (breaking) incurs any obligation, whether the hanth occurs while still a disbeliever or after accepting Islam. Additionally, kaffarah is an act of worship ('ibadah), and a disbeliever is not considered qualified for worship in a manner that would make kaffarah obligatory. Thus, no kaffarah is required upon hanth. This ruling applies regardless of when the hanth occurs; the invalidity originates from the moment of the oath.

Key Terms
كافر (kafir): A disbeliever who does not affirm the oneness of Allah and the prophethood.
تعظیم (ta'zim): Glorification and reverence of Allah, a necessary element in an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A disbeliever swears 'By Allah, I will not eat pork' and then breaks this oath after accepting Islam. What is the ruling?
A) He must pay kaffarah.
B) He must perform ghusl.
C) Nothing is obligatory upon him.
D) The oath is valid and he must repent.

Key Takeaway
An oath from a disbeliever is void from the outset, and no kaffarah is required upon breaking it, even if the person later becomes Muslim.

When Saying 'Haram' Becomes an Oath
Dispute on whether 'haram' constitutes an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school treats saying 'haram' about one's own property as an oath, and distinguish the Shafi'i counterargument.

Core Idea
Saying 'haram' on your own property creates an oath, not intrinsic prohibition.

In this passage, we examine a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over whether a person who says 'This is haram upon me' about something he owns incurs any legal obligation. The Hanafi school holds that the statement is an oath, while the Shafi'i school considers it invalid. Understanding this difference requires analyzing how each school interprets the word 'haram' and the principles of oath-making.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the thing does not become intrinsically haram, but the statement is an oath. If the person uses the thing, he must pay kaffarah of yamin.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State that Imam al-Shafi'i holds no kaffarah is due because declaring halal haram is a reversal of Shari'ah and cannot constitute a valid Shar'i transaction.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain the Shafi'i reasoning: the oath is a valid Shar'i transaction, but this statement is invalid because it attempts to reverse God's law, so it cannot be given any effect.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Show how the Hanafis rebut this: the word 'haram' can be interpreted as establishing prohibition in the sense of an oath, preserving its linguistic effect without declaring the thing intrinsically haram.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, the speaker owes kaffarah if he uses the thing; in the Shafi'i school, no obligation arises at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ حَرَّمَ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ شَيْئًا مِمَّا يَمْلِكُهُ لَمْ يَصِرُ مُحَرَّمًا وَعَلَيْهِ إِنِ اسْتَبَاحَهُ كَفَّارَةُ يَمِيْنِ وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ كَفَّارَةَ عَلَيْهِ لِاَنَّ تَحْرِيمَ الْحَلَالِ قَلْبُ الْمَشْرُوعِ فَلَايَنْعَقِدُ بِهِ تَصَرُّفَ مَشْرُوعٌ وَهُوَ الْيَمِينُ وَلَنَا أَنَّ اللَّفْظَ نَبِى عَنْ الْبَاتِ الْحُرْمَةِ وَقَدْ اَمْكَنَ اِعْمَالُهُ بِبُوتِ الْحُرْمَةِ لِغَيْرِهِ بِالْبَاتِ مُوْجِبِ الْيَمِينِ فَيُصَارُ إِلَيْهِ ثُمَّ ذَا فَعَلَ مِمَّا حَرَّمَهُ قَلِيلًا اَوْ كَثِيرًا حَيْتَ وَوَجَبَتِ الْكَفَّارَةُ وَهُوَ الْمَعْنى مِنَ الْإِسْتِبَاحَةِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ لَانَّ تَحْرِيمَ إِذَا ثَبَتَ تَنَاوَلَ كُلَّ جُزْءٍ مِنْهُ ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے اپنے اوپر ایسی چیز حرام کر لی جس کا وہ مالک ہے تو وہ چیز اس کی اس وجہ سے اس کی اپنی ذات پر حرام نہیں ہوگی۔ لیکن اگر وہ اس چیز کے ساتھ مباح اور جائز جیسا تعلق رکھے تو اس پر کفارہ قسم لازم آئے گا۔ اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا ہے کہ اس پر کفارہ نہیں ہے کیونکہ حلال کو حرام کرلینا حکم کو الٹ دینا ہے اس لئے یہ منعقد نہیں ہوگی کوئی شرعی تصرف ہے۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ لفظ سے حرمت ثابت کرنا نکلتا ہے۔ اور اس کے موافق عمل کرنا ممکن ہے۔ یعنی جب اس نے کہا کہ یہ چیز مجھ پر حرام ہے تو کسی مخالفت کے بعد بھی اس پر اس طرح عمل کرنا ممکن ہے کہ حرمت ثابت کی جائے مگر اس کی حرمت ذاتی نہیں ہوگی بلکہ قسم کے تقاضا کے مطابق اسے یہ قسم قرار دیا جائے گا۔ پھر اس نے جس چیز کو حرام کیا ہے اگر اسے تھوڑا یا بہت کر لیا تو وہ قسم ٹوٹ گئی اور اب اس پر کفارہ لازم آ گیا۔
And whoever declares haram upon himself something that he owns, it does not become haram, and if he makes use of it (istibahah), he owes the kaffarah of an oath. Al-Shafi'i said: No kaffarah is due on him, because declaring a lawful thing haram is reversing the Shari'ah, so no valid Shar'i transaction is concluded, including an oath. Our proof is that the word conveys the establishment of prohibition, and it is possible to act upon it by establishing a prohibition for something else by means of an oath's consequence. So it is applied accordingly. Then if he does of what he declared haram, little or much, he has committed hanth and kaffarah becomes obligatory. This is the meaning of 'istibahah' mentioned, because if prohibition is established, it covers every part of it. Translation: And if someone declares haram upon himself something that he owns, that thing does not become haram upon him due to this statement. But if he engages with that thing in a permissible manner, then kaffarah of an oath will become obligatory upon him. Imam al-Shafi'i said: No kaffarah is due upon him, because making halal haram is reversing the ruling, so a valid Shar'i transaction, which is the oath, is not concluded. Our proof is that from the word (haram) the establishment of prohibition is derived, and acting upon it is possible. That is, when he said 'This thing is haram upon me,' it is possible to act upon it after any opposition in the following way: the prohibition is established, but it will not be intrinsic (dhati) prohibition; rather, according to the requirement of an oath, this statement will be considered an oath. Then if he does that which he declared haram, little or much, the oath is broken and now kaffarah becomes obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute about the legal effect of saying 'this is haram upon me' regarding something one owns. The Hanafi position: the thing does not become intrinsically haram (like pork), but the statement is treated as an oath (yamin). Therefore, if the person later uses or consumes that thing, he has broken the oath (hanth) and must pay kaffarah of yamin (feeding ten poor, etc.). The Shafi'i view: such a statement is not valid as an oath because declaring halal as haram is a reversal of Allah's law; thus no legal effect whatsoever, not even an oath. The Hanafis argue that the word 'haram' can be used to express prohibition in a relative sense (i.e., for oneself due to an oath), as is common in the language. They rely on the principle that the word should be given effect if possible. Here, it is possible to interpret it as an oath (yamin) that prohibits the thing for the speaker in the sense that if he uses it, he incurs kaffarah. Thus, the ruling does not make the thing haram in itself, but imposes an obligation (wajib) to avoid it as a matter of the oath; if he breaks it, kaffarah is due. This is a practical consequence: the speaker is sinning if he uses it without kaffarah.

Key Terms
حرام (haram): When said by a person about his own property, it does not make the thing intrinsically haram but acts as an oath.
کفارہ قسم (kaffarah yamin): The expiation for breaking an oath: feeding ten poor, clothing them, or freeing a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says, 'This bread is haram upon me,' according to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if he later eats the bread?
A) The bread becomes haram and he must repent.
B) He owes kaffarah of an oath.
C) Nothing is required; the statement is meaningless.
D) He must perform ghusl.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'haram' on your own property creates an oath; using the property triggers kaffarah, not intrinsic prohibition.

Ruling on Vows: Absolute, Conditional, and the Distinction with Oaths
Ruling with proof: absolute and conditional vows, with intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on absolute and conditional vows, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding conditional vows with undesired conditions, and identify when a conditional vow becomes an oath with a choice.

Core Idea
A vow is wajib; but if the condition is disliked, the vow becomes an oath—you may choose expiation.

Vows (nadhr) are promises to perform an act of worship. The Hanafi school distinguishes between absolute vows and conditional ones. But what if the condition is something you don't want to happen? In this lesson, we examine the primary proofs and a key intra-Hanafi dispute that gives you a choice between fulfilling the vow or paying expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for absolute vows
From the text: 'نذر مطلق...فعليه الوفاء' – an absolute vow (without condition) must be fulfilled. Note that the ruling is wajib, not fard, because the proof is from an ahad hadith.
2
Examine the proof (dalil)
The hadith 'man nadhara wa samma fa'alayhi al-wafa'' is cited. Analyse the imperative 'alayhi al-wafa' which indicates obligation in Hanafi usul. Also note that the hadith is zanni al-thubut, hence the ruling is wajib.
3
Analyze the initial Hanafi view on conditional vows
The text initially states that a conditional vow is like an absolute vow (according to Abu Hanifa's first view), so fulfillment is required. This is based on the generality of the hadith and the principle that a conditional promise is like an unconditional one.
4
Identify the retraction and the correct view
Imam Abu Hanifa retracted (raja'a) and agreed with Imam Muhammad: if the condition is disliked (e.g., drinking wine), the phrasing contains the meaning of an oath (abstention), giving the person a choice between fulfilling the vow or giving expiation. If the condition is desired (e.g., recovery from illness), it remains a pure vow.
The text concludes 'هذا التفصيل هو الصحيح' – this detail is correct.
5
Apply the distinction
Given a scenario: 'If I smoke, I will fast a month' (smoking is disliked). The person has a choice: fast the month or give kaffarat yamin. Contrast: 'If Allah heals my son, I will give $100' – only fulfillment is valid.

Source Text
